We can try to characterize f(N) in terms of the divisors. Let the divisors of N be: 1 = d_1 < d_2 < ... < d_k = N. Then the proper divisors are d_1,...,d_{k-1}. The three largest proper divisors are d_{k-3}, d_{k-2}, d_{k-1} (if k-1 ≥ 3). So f(N) = d_{k-3}+d_{k-2}+d_{k-1}. But note that d_{k-1} = N/ d_2 (since d_2 is the smallest prime factor p). Actually the pairing: d_i * d_{k+1-i} = N. So d_{k-1} = N/d_2, d_{k-2} = N/d_3, d_{k-3} = N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4, where d_2, d_3, d_4 are the three smallest divisors greater than 1? Actually d_2 is the smallest prime factor (since any divisor >1 includes the smallest prime factor). d_3 is the next smallest divisor; it could be either p^2 (if p^2|N) or q (next prime factor). d_4 is the next after that. So f(N) = N * (1/d_2 + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4). This is an integer because each term is a divisor.

Thus we have f(N) = N * (1/p + 1/q + 1/r) where p, q, r are the three smallest divisors >1? But careful: d_2 = smallest prime factor p. d_3 = next smallest divisor; d_4 = next. But we need to ensure that these are indeed the three smallest divisors greater than 1. However, note that 1 is also a divisor, but we exclude 1 because we need proper divisors? Actually d_1=1, d_2=p, d_3, d_4. So the three largest proper divisors are N/d_2, N/d_3, N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4. So f(N) = N * (1/p + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4). But d_3 and d_4 are not necessarily primes; they could be powers of p or other primes. However, note that if N has a divisor d such that 1<d< p? Not possible because p is the smallest. So d_2 = p. d_3 is either p^2 (if p^2|N) or the next smallest prime factor q (if p^2 does not divide N). Similarly d_4 is either p^3, or p*q, or q^2, or next prime r, etc. So the expression depends on the factorization.

The condition that the sequence is infinite is that for all n, a_n has at least 3 proper divisors, i.e., a_n is composite and not a prime square? Actually numbers like 12 have many proper divisors, okay. But we need to avoid hitting a number with exactly 2 proper divisors (p^2) or 1 proper divisor (prime). Also p*q with p,q distinct primes: divisors 1,p,q,pq, proper:1,p,q (3 proper divisors). So those are in D. Now, we need to determine all a1 such that the iterates f^k(a1) are all in D. The function f is defined for N in D. f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. We have seen that many numbers eventually map to a fixed point in the family F = {6m: m odd, 5∤m}. But also there might be numbers that map to another number in F after multiple steps. Could there be numbers that map into a cycle that is not a fixed point? Let's analyze the function more systematically.

We need to characterize f(N) in terms of the smallest divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Let d_2 = p. Let d_3 be the next smallest divisor >1. Let d_4 be the next. Then f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4 = N * (1/p + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4). Note that d_3 and d_4 are divisors that are at least p^2 or q (if p^2 does not divide N). In fact, the set of divisors greater than 1 is sorted: p, then either p^2 if p^2|N, otherwise the next prime factor q. Then d_4 is either p^3, or p*q, or q^2, or next prime r, etc. So f(N) is an integer.

We can try to bound f(N) relative to N. Since 1/p + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4 ≤ 1/2 + 1/3 + 1/4 = 13/12 ≈ 1.08333. So f(N) ≤ (13/12) N. Also, since d_3 ≥ p and d_4 ≥ p? Actually d_3 ≥ p, but could be equal to p if p^2? No, d_3 could be p^2 if p^2|N, but p^2 > p unless p=1? So d_3 > p. So 1/d_3 < 1/p. So f(N) < N*(1/p+1/p+1/p) = N * (3/p) ≤ N * (3/2) = 1.5N. But better bound: Since d_3 ≥ p+? Not necessarily; if p=2, d_3 could be 3 or 4. So 1/p+1/d_3+1/d_4 ≤ 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12. So f(N) ≤ 13N/12. Also, we have a lower bound: Since d_2, d_3, d_4 are at most N? Actually they are divisors, so d_2 ≥ 2, d_3 ≥ 3, d_4 ≥ 4? Not always: If N is a prime power, say N = p^k, then the divisors are 1, p, p^2, ..., p^k. Then d_2 = p, d_3 = p^2, d_4 = p^3. Then f(N) = N/p + N/p^2 + N/p^3 = N*(1/p+1/p^2+1/p^3). For p=2, that's N*(1/2+1/4+1/8)=7N/8 < N. So f(N) can be less than N. For p=3, f(N)=N*(1/3+1/9+1/27)= (9+3+1)/27 N = 13N/27 < N. So f(N) can be significantly less than N.

Thus the function can both increase and decrease. But we need sequences that never hit a number with <3 proper divisors.

Suppose N is not in D? No, we need D. Let's try to see if any number maps to itself other than F. That would be a fixed point. Are there fixed points besides F? Solve N = N/p + N/q + N/r with p,q,r the three smallest divisors >1. For N to be a fixed point, we need 1/p+1/q+1/r = 1. For p=2, q=3, r=6: 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, so that works. We want to know when f(N) is in F. Since F consists of numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, i.e., numbers of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5. So f(N) must be a multiple of 6, and f(N) mod 4 ≠ 0, and f(N) mod 5 ≠ 0.

Maybe we can find a more structural approach: Consider the function g(N) = N - f(N) = N - (sum of three largest proper divisors). Note that the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r with p,q,r the three smallest divisors >1. So g(N) = N(1 - 1/p - 1/q - 1/r). For N to be a fixed point, g(N)=0. For N to eventually become fixed, the iterates must eventually satisfy g=0. Could there be a cycle where g is not zero but the iteration cycles? Let's examine the behavior of f.

For example, N=5*7=35, f=7+5+1=13 not multiple of 6; N=5*11=55, f=11+5+1=17; N=7*11=77, f=11+7+1=19; N=5*7*11=385, f=77+55+35=167; N=5*7*13=455, f=91+65+35=191; N=5*7*17=595, f=119+85+35=239; all not multiples of 6. Could there be a case where the sum is a multiple of 6? Let's try N=5*7*? Actually if N is a product of distinct odd primes, f = pq+pr+qr. For this to be divisible by 6, it must be even and divisible by 3. Since p,q,r are odd, pq is odd, pr odd, qr odd, sum of three odds is odd, so not even. So cannot be even. So for N odd, not divisible by 3, f(N) is odd, so not a multiple of 6. Thus f(N) is never a multiple of 6 for odd N coprime to 6. Therefore, the only way to get a multiple of 6 is if N is even. So the sequence from any odd number cannot produce a multiple of 6. And the fixed points are multiples of 6. So if the sequence ever hits an odd number that is not a fixed point, it can never become a fixed point because it would never become a multiple of 6. The only odd numbers in D are those coprime to 6, and they map to odd numbers (since f(N) is odd). So they stay odd. So the only way to get a fixed point (which is even) is to start with an even number. So a1 must be even.

Maybe we can characterize the sequence in terms of the exponents of 2 and 3. Since f(N) depends on the smallest divisors, we can try to track the 2-adic valuation and the 3-adic valuation.

We should write a complete rigorous proof.

Let's produce the proof.

We'll start with definitions and lemmas.

We need to be careful with the case when the number is a prime power? For numbers with at least three proper divisors, they are composite and not prime squares. For numbers divisible by 6, they are composite. So the analysis holds.

Now, let's craft the proof.

**Proof:**

Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\) (provided \(N\) has at least three proper divisors). The sequence is defined by \(a_1, a_2, \dots\) with \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for \(n\ge 1\), and each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors.

We determine all possible values of \(a_1\) such that the sequence is infinite.

**Step 1: Odd terms cannot occur in an infinite sequence.**

If \(N\) is odd and has at least three proper divisors, let \(p\) be its smallest prime factor. Then \(p\ge 3\). The three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\) are \(p\), \(q\), \(r\) with \(1<p<q<r\). The three largest proper divisors are \(N/p, N/q, N/r\). Hence
\[
f(N)=N\left(\frac1p+\frac1q+\frac1r\right).
\]
Since \(p\ge 3\), we have
\[
\frac1p+\frac1q+\frac1r\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac17=\frac{71}{105}<1,
\]
so \(f(N)<N\). Moreover, \(f(N)\) is an integer. Thus for odd \(N\) the function \(f\) is strictly decreasing. Consequently, if some term \(a_n\) is odd, the sequence \(a_n, a_{n+1}, a_{n+2},\dots\) is strictly decreasing and must eventually become a prime or a square of a prime, because the only way the iteration can stop (i.e., the term has fewer than three proper divisors) is when the term is a prime or a prime square. But the problem requires every term to have at least three proper divisors, so the sequence would be forced to terminate. Hence an infinite sequence cannot contain any odd term. Therefore all terms must be even.

**Step 2: All terms must be divisible by 3.**

Assume that a term \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\). Write \(N=2^a\cdot M\) with \(M\) odd and not divisible by \(3\). Let \(p\) be the smallest prime factor of \(M\) (so \(p\ge 5\)). Then the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\) are \(2,4\) (if \(a\ge2\)) or \(2,p\) (if \(a=1\)); in either case the third divisor is at least \(4\) or \(p\ge5\). Hence
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{p}\quad\text{if }a\ge2,\qquad
f(N)=M+\frac{2M}{p}+\frac{2M}{r}\quad\text{if }a=1,
\]
where \(r\) is the next divisor after \(p\). In the first case, \(f(N)=2^{a-2}M\left(3+\frac4p\right)\), which is odd exactly when \(a=2\) (because then \(2^{0}M\cdot(3+4/p)\) is odd). If \(a\ge3\), then \(f(N)\) is even but not divisible by \(3\) (since \(3+4/p\) is not a multiple of \(3\)). Repeating the argument, the exponent of \(2\) decreases by \(2\) each time while the number remains even and not divisible by \(3\); eventually the exponent becomes \(2\) or \(1\), at which point the image becomes odd. Thus any sequence that ever contains an even number not divisible by \(3\) will eventually produce an odd term, which then leads to a finite sequence as argued in Step 1. Therefore for the sequence to be infinite, every term must be divisible by \(3\). In particular, \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(3\) and also all iterates.

Combining with Step 1, we see that \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

**Step 3: Classification of numbers that are multiples of 6.**

Let \(a_1=2^a\cdot3^b\cdot M\) with \(M\) coprime to \(6\). Since \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), we have \(a\ge1\), \(b\ge1\). The three smallest divisors of \(a_1\) greater than \(1\) are:
\[
2,\quad 3,\quad d = 
\begin{cases}
4 & \text{if } a\ge 2,\\
5 & \text{if } a=1 \text{ and } 5\mid a_1,\\
6 & \text{if } a=1 \text{ and } 5\nmid a_1.
\end{cases}
\]
Consequently,
\[
f(a_1)=
\begin{cases}
\frac{13}{12}a_1 & \text{if } a\ge 2,\\[4pt]
\frac{31}{30}a_1 & \text{if } a=1,\ 5\mid a_1,\\[4pt]
a_1 & \text{if } a=1,\ 5\nmid a_1.
\end{cases}
\]

- **Case 1:** \(a=1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\). Then \(f(a_1)=a_1\), so the sequence is constant. Since \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), it has at least three proper divisors (e.g., \(1,2,3\) for \(a_1=6\)). Hence every such \(a_1\) yields an infinite sequence. These are precisely the numbers \(a_1\) such that \(a_1/6\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\). In terms of the exponent of \(2\), this means \(v_2(a_1)=1\) (odd) and \(v_2(a_1)\le 2v_3(a_1)-1\) is automatically true because \(v_3(a_1)\ge1\) gives \(1\le 2v_3-1\) iff \(v_3\ge1\). Also the condition that \(5\nmid a_1\) is exactly the condition that \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(5\).

- **Case 2:** \(a=1\) and \(5\mid a_1\). Then \(f(a_1)=\frac{31}{30}a_1\). Write \(a_1=2\cdot3^b\cdot5\cdot U\) with \(U\) coprime to \(6\). Then \(f(a_1)=31\cdot3^{b-1}U\). This number is odd and not divisible by \(3\) if \(b=1\), or divisible by \(3\) if \(b\ge2\). In either case it is odd, so by Step 1 the sequence will eventually become odd and then strictly decreasing, leading to a term with fewer than three proper divisors. Therefore no such \(a_1\) can give an infinite sequence.

- **Case 3:** \(a\ge 2\). Then \(f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1\). For the sequence to stay infinite, we need \(f(a_1)\) to be even and divisible by \(3\) (so that the next iteration can continue). Since \(f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1\), it is even exactly when \(a_1/12\) is even, i.e., when \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(24\). Hence we require \(v_2(a_1)\ge3\). Then let
\[
v_2(a_1)=a,\qquad v_3(a_1)=b.
\]
Because \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(12\), we have \(a\ge2\), \(b\ge1\). After one step we obtain
\[
a_2=\frac{13}{12}a_1,\qquad v_2(a_2)=a-2,\quad v_3(a_2)=b-1.
\]
Repeating this, after \(k\) steps we have
\[
a_k=\frac{13^k}{12^k}a_1,\qquad v_2(a_k)=a-2k,\quad v_3(a_k)=b-k,
\]
as long as \(a-2k\ge2\) and \(b-k\ge1\). The iteration continues while these inequalities hold. In order for the sequence to remain infinite, we must eventually reach a number with \(v_2=1\) (so that the third divisor becomes \(6\) and the function becomes the identity) and also the number must not be divisible by \(5\) (so that the third divisor is not \(5\)). The exponent of \(2\) decreases by \(2\) each step; therefore after \(t\) steps we have \(v_2=1\) precisely when \(t=(a-1)/2\). For this to be an integer, \(a\) must be odd. So we need \(v_2(a_1)\) odd. Moreover, at that step we require \(v_3\) still at least \(1\): \(b-t\ge1\) gives \(b\ge t+1 = (a-1)/2+1 = (a+1)/2\). Hence \(a\le 2b-1\). Finally, the number obtained after \(t\) steps is
\[
a_{t+1}=\frac{13^t}{12^t}a_1,
\]
and since \(13\) and \(12\) are not multiples of \(5\), it is divisible by \(5\) iff \(a_1\) is divisible by \(5\). To have the next step be the identity (i.e., \(f(a_{t+1})=a_{t+1}\)), we need that \(a_{t+1}\) is not divisible by \(5\). Therefore \(a_1\) must not be divisible by \(5\). Under these conditions, the iteration stabilises at \(a_{t+1}\), which is a fixed point (and it is a multiple of \(6\) with no factor \(5\), so it indeed has at least three proper divisors). Hence any \(a_1\) satisfying
\[
6\mid a_1,\quad 5\nmid a_1,\quad v_2(a_1)\text{ odd},\quad v_2(a_1)\le 2v_3(a_1)-1,
\]
produces an infinite sequence.

Conversely, if \(a_1\) produces an infinite sequence, then all terms are even and divisible by \(3\) (by Steps 1 and 2). The case \(a_1\) with \(v_2(a_1)=1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\) is already covered. If \(v_2(a_1)\) is even, then after repeated application of \(f\) we would eventually obtain a number with \(v_2=2\), which gives an odd next term, contradicting the fact that the sequence stays even. Hence \(v_2(a_1)\) must be odd. Also, if \(v_2(a_1) > 2v_3(a_1)-1\), then after \((v_2-1)/2\) steps the \(3\)-adic valuation becomes zero, and the number becomes even and not divisible by \(3\); then as argued, such a number eventually leads to an odd term, again a contradiction. Therefore the necessary conditions are exactly the ones listed.

**Conclusion:** The possible values of \(a_1\) are precisely the positive integers that are multiples of \(6\) and not divisible by \(5\), whose exponent of \(2\) is odd and does not exceed twice the exponent of \(3\) minus one. In other words,
\[
a_1 = 2^{2k+1}\cdot 3^{m}\cdot t,
\]
where \(k,m\) are non‑negative integers, \(m\ge k+1\), and \(t\) is coprime to \(6\) and not divisible by \(5\).

Now, let's craft the proof text clearly.

We'll use standard notation: v2(N) = exponent of 2 in N, etc.

- Definition of f.
- Lemma 1: If N is odd, then f(N) < N and f(N) integer.

- Lemma 2: If N is even and not divisible by 3, then the sequence eventually becomes odd. Proof via valuations.

- Therefore, all terms must be divisible by 3, so a1 is multiple of 6.

- Now, let a1 = 2^a * 3^b * M, M coprime to 6. Compute f(a1) as above.

- Case a=1, 5∤M: constant sequence, works.

Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\), defined whenever \(N\) has at least three proper divisors. The sequence is given by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for \(n\ge 1\) and each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors. We determine all \(a_1\) for which the sequence is infinite.

**Step 1: Odd terms cannot occur in an infinite sequence.**  
If \(N\) is odd and has at least three proper divisors, let \(p\) be its smallest prime factor. Then \(p\ge 3\). The three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\) are \(p,q,r\) with \(1<p<q<r\); the three largest proper divisors are \(N/p,N/q,N/r\). Hence  
\[
f(N)=N\left(\frac1p+\frac1q+\frac1r\right).
\]  
Since \(p\ge 3\), we have  
\[
\frac1p+\frac1q+\frac1r\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac17=\frac{71}{105}<1,
\]  
so \(f(N)<N\). Moreover, \(f(N)\) is an integer. Thus for odd \(N\) the function \(f\) is strictly decreasing. Consequently, if some term \(a_n\) is odd, the sequence \(a_n,a_{n+1},\dots\) is strictly decreasing and must eventually become a prime or a square of a prime, because the only way the iteration can stop (i.e., the term has fewer than three proper divisors) is when the term is a prime or a prime square. But the problem requires every term to have at least three proper divisors, so the sequence would be forced to terminate. Hence an infinite sequence cannot contain any odd term. Therefore all terms are even.

**Step 2: All terms must be divisible by \(3\).**  
Assume that a term \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(3\). Write \(N=2^a\cdot M\) with \(M\) odd and not divisible by \(3\). Let \(p\) be the smallest prime factor of \(M\) (so \(p\ge 5\)). Then the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\) are \(2\), \(4\) (if \(a\ge 2\)) or \(2,p\) (if \(a=1\)); in either case the third divisor is at least \(4\) or \(p\ge 5\). Hence  
\[
f(N)=\begin{cases}
\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{p}=\frac{2^{a-2}M(3+4/p)}{} & \text{if }a\ge2,\\[6pt]
M+\frac{2M}{p}+\frac{2M}{r} & \text{if }a=1,
\end{cases}
\]  
where \(r\) is the next divisor after \(p\). In the first case \(f(N)\) is odd exactly when \(a=2\) (because then \(2^{0}M(3+4/p)\) is odd). If \(a\ge3\), then \(f(N)\) is even but not divisible by \(3\) (since \(3+4/p\) is not a multiple of \(3\)). Repeating the argument, the exponent of \(2\) decreases by \(2\) each time while the number remains even and not divisible by \(3\); eventually the exponent becomes \(2\) or \(1\), at which point the image becomes odd. Thus any sequence that ever contains an even number not divisible by \(3\) will eventually produce an odd term, which then leads to a finite sequence as argued in Step 1. Therefore for the sequence to be infinite, every term must be divisible by \(3\). In particular, \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(3\) and also all iterates. Combined with Step 1, we see that \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

**Step 3: Classification of numbers that are multiples of \(6\).**  
Let \(a_1=2^a\cdot3^b\cdot M\) with \(M\) coprime to \(6\). Since \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), we have \(a\ge1\), \(b\ge1\). The three smallest divisors of \(a_1\) greater than \(1\) are  
\[
2,\quad 3,\quad d = 
\begin{cases}
4 & \text{if } a\ge 2,\\
5 & \text{if } a=1 \text{ and } 5\mid a_1,\\
6 & \text{if } a=1 \text{ and } 5\nmid a_1.
\end{cases}
\]  
Consequently,  
\[
f(a_1)=
\begin{cases}
\frac{13}{12}a_1 & \text{if } a\ge 2,\\[4pt]
\frac{31}{30}a_1 & \text{if } a=1,\ 5\mid a_1,\\[4pt]
a_1 & \text{if } a=1,\ 5\nmid a_1.
\end{cases}
\]

- **Case 1:** \(a=1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\). Then \(f(a_1)=a_1\), so the sequence is constant. Since \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\), it has at least three proper divisors (e.g., \(1,2,3\) for \(a_1=6\)). Hence every such \(a_1\) yields an infinite sequence. These are precisely the numbers \(a_1\) such that \(a_1/6\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\). In terms of the exponent of \(2\), this means \(v_2(a_1)=1\) (odd) and \(v_2(a_1)\le 2v_3(a_1)-1\) is automatically true because \(v_3(a_1)\ge1\) gives \(1\le 2v_3-1\) iff \(v_3\ge1\). Also the condition that \(5\nmid a_1\) is exactly the condition that \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(5\).

- **Case 2:** \(a=1\) and \(5\mid a_1\). Then \(f(a_1)=\frac{31}{30}a_1\). Write \(a_1=2\cdot3^b\cdot5\cdot U\) with \(U\) coprime to \(6\). Then \(f(a_1)=31\cdot3^{b-1}U\). This number is odd and not divisible by \(3\) if \(b=1\), or divisible by \(3\) if \(b\ge2\). In either case it is odd, so by Step 1 the sequence will eventually become odd and then strictly decreasing, leading to a term with fewer than three proper divisors. Therefore no such \(a_1\) can give an infinite sequence.

- **Case 3:** \(a\ge 2\). Then \(f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1\). For the sequence to stay infinite, we need \(f(a_1)\) to be even and divisible by \(3\) (so that the next iteration can continue). Since \(f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1\), it is even exactly when \(a_1/12\) is even, i.e., when \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(24\). Hence we require \(v_2(a_1)\ge3\). Then let  
\[
v_2(a_1)=a,\qquad v_3(a_1)=b.
\]  
Because \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(12\), we have \(a\ge2\), \(b\ge1\). After one step we obtain  
\[
a_2=\frac{13}{12}a_1,\qquad v_2(a_2)=a-2,\quad v_3(a_2)=b-1.
\]  
Repeating this, after \(k\) steps we have  
\[
a_k=\frac{13^k}{12^k}a_1,\qquad v_2(a_k)=a-2k,\quad v_3(a_k)=b-k,
\]  
as long as \(a-2k\ge2\) and \(b-k\ge1\). The iteration continues while these inequalities hold. In order for the sequence to remain infinite, we must eventually reach a number with \(v_2=1\) (so that the third divisor becomes \(6\) and the function becomes the identity) and also the number must not be divisible by \(5\) (so that the third divisor is not \(5\)). The exponent of \(2\) decreases by \(2\) each step; therefore after \(t\) steps we have \(v_2=1\) precisely when \(t=(a-1)/2\). For this to be an integer, \(a\) must be odd. So we need \(v_2(a_1)\) odd. Moreover, at that step we require \(v_3\) still at least \(1\): \(b-t\ge1\) gives \(b\ge t+1 = (a-1)/2+1 = (a+1)/2\). Hence \(a\le 2b-1\). Finally, the number obtained after \(t\) steps is  
\[
a_{t+1}=\frac{13^t}{12^t}a_1,
\]  
and since \(13\) and \(12\) are not multiples of \(5\), it is divisible by \(5\) iff \(a_1\) is divisible by \(5\). To have the next step be the identity (i.e., \(f(a_{t+1})=a_{t+1}\)), we need that \(a_{t+1}\) is not divisible by \(5\). Therefore \(a_1\) must not be divisible by \(5\). Under these conditions, the iteration stabilises at \(a_{t+1}\), which is a fixed point (and it is a multiple of \(6\) with no factor \(5\), so it indeed has at least three proper divisors). Hence any \(a_1\) satisfying  
\[
6\mid a_1,\quad 5\nmid a_1,\quad v_2(a_1)\text{ odd},\quad v_2(a_1)\le 2v_3(a_1)-1,
\]  
produces an infinite sequence.

Conversely, if \(a_1\) produces an infinite sequence, then all terms are even and divisible by \(3\) (by Steps 1 and 2). The case \(a_1\) with \(v_2(a_1)=1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\) is already covered. If \(v_2(a_1)\) is even, then after repeated application of \(f\) we would eventually obtain a number with \(v_2=2\), which gives an odd next term, contradicting the fact that the sequence stays even. Hence \(v_2(a_1)\) must be odd. Also, if \(v_2(a_1) > 2v_3(a_1)-1\), then after \((v_2-1)/2\) steps the \(3\)-adic valuation becomes zero, and the number becomes even and not divisible by \(3\); then as argued, such a number eventually leads to an odd term, again a contradiction. Therefore the necessary conditions are exactly the ones listed.

**Conclusion:** The possible values of \(a_1\) are precisely the positive integers that are multiples of \(6\) and not divisible by \(5\), whose exponent of \(2\) is odd and does not exceed twice the exponent of \(3\) minus one. In other words,
\[
a_1 = 2^{2k+1}\cdot 3^{m}\cdot t,
\]
where \(k,m\) are non‑negative integers, \(m\ge k+1\), and \(t\) is coprime to \(6\) and not divisible by \(5\).
